Preface
This thesis has been performed for five months containing 30 ETCS points at the De-
partment of Civil Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark, DTU, in partial
fulfillment for the degree Master of Science in Engineering, MSc Eng in Architectural
Engineering. The work has been completed in the spring semester of 2022.
An acknowledge to the supervising team of the project: Finn Ehmsen, Finn Larsen, Arne
Egerup, and Jørgen Munch-Andersen. The frequent meetings, support and availability
during the project has been greatly valued.
The tested joints with punched metal plate fasteners were sponsored by Palsgaard Spær,
which made the tests at DTU possible, and for this, a great gratitude should be said.
Continually, a thank you to the staff at both the structural laboratory at DTU and the
test house of BUILD for essential support to make the tests feasible.
Benedikte Kirkegaard Breilev
Date
ii
Withdrawal of punched metal plate fasteners in trussed rafters
30-06-2022
==PAGE:5==
Abstract
Withdrawal of punched metal plate fasteners in trussed rafters is registered multiple times
in Denmark. Roof constructions with punched metal plate fasteners are common in Den-
mark, hence a need for knowledge of the causes has occurred. Based on observations and
theory, it is suspected that withdrawal of punched metal plate fasteners occurs in extreme
temperatures or relative humidity in attics. Three tests have been performed to investi-
gate the conditions in roof constructions with reduced ventilation, and test timber joints
exposed to respectively varying temperatures and relative humidity. A large reduction of
ventilation was not obtained, since the conditions in the attic did not reach critical or
extreme temperatures or relative humidity. The tests of timber joints did obtain a small
withdrawal of punched metal plate fasteners, but the reasons are not fully mapped, since
the test results were varying in terms of the type of joint, density of the wood, and type of
punched metal plate fastener. Though, all withdrawal happened at joints with small gaps
at the beginning.
Withdrawal of punched metal plate fasteners in trussed rafters

Withdrawal of punched metal plate fasteners in trussed rafters
==PAGE:9==
1
Introduction
Prefabricated elements such as doors, walls, and rafters, are a huge part of building a
house [19]. Typically, prefabricated rafters are connected with punched metal plate fasten-
ers, PMPF. These structures are optimized, hence a low material consumption but with
great strength, why the elements and joints are of high importance. Therefore, PMPF is
widely spread and used in Denmark, which increases the interest in knowing more about
the problem of withdrawal and why it occurs. Problems with the withdrawal of PMPF
have been registered for example in Denmark, but also in countries with similar weather
conditions. It is not a commonly known problem, hence no systematic overview occurs in
Denmark. Since the distribution of PMPF is wide, it is seen of high importance to inves-
tigate the reasons for the withdrawal, hence special humidity conditions or temperatures
are suspected to influence the withdrawal of PMPF. Wood is an organic material that is
affected by relative humidity and temperature, hence it shrinks and expands as the tem-
perature and RH vary. The trussed rafters are connected with the PMPF, hence a material
that especially are influenced by temperature thinking of volume change. The interest in
wood as a building material has been increasing as the focus on sustainable buildings has
been increasing. Understanding the material and its properties have therefore been a huge
motivation.
1.1
Problem statement
The project aims to investigate, map, and evaluate on withdrawal of PMPF. The hy-
pothesis is that withdrawal could be caused by varying temperatures and RH since some
registrations indicate that. Therefore, the present project aims to achieve as much in-
formation on the conditions in attics, and expose timber joints connected with PMPF to
different temperatures and RH. Hence, the research questions to answer are mainly:
• How are the temperature and RH conditions in attics at varying ventilation?
• How is the MC affected by the conditions in attics?
• How affect temperature variation withdrawal of PMPF?
• How affect RH variation withdrawal of PMPF?
To answer the large question:
• Why does the withdrawal of PMPF occur for trussed rafters in attics?
Withdrawal of punched metal plate fasteners in trussed rafters
1
==PAGE:10==
1.2
Outline of report
The scope of the project contains both tests of reduced ventilation and tests of timber
joints connected with PMPF. First, the known, existing cases of withdrawal of PMPF
have been mapped as well as experiences with performing tests of respective joints with
PMPF and ventilated roof construction in BUILD. Then the required material physics of
respectively wood and steel are elaborated, hence it contains an explanation of temperature,
RH, and MC and the considerations in terms of volume change. An overall presentation of
roof structures and corresponding timber joints occurs just as the ventilation of the attics
and the weather conditions in Denmark. When the basis of knowledge is established, the
methods of performing the three tests are designed and adjusted to obtain the goal and the
available facilities. Test 1 is at BUILD, where ventilation of attics is investigated. Tests 2
and 3 are performed at DTU, and consist of timber joints connected with PMPF exposed
to respectively varying temperatures and RH. The results from the tests are presented and
analyzed to evaluate them in the end.
2
Withdrawal of punched metal plate fasteners in trussed rafters
==PAGE:11==
2
Literature study
In the following section, knowledge about existing cases with withdrawn punched metal
plate fasteners, PMPF, are mapped based on both information from experts, registrations,
and reports of observations of which possible reasons are presented. Additionally, a study
of tests regarding withdrawal of PMPF is done, hence learning can be drawn from the
experiences of others. Further, a similar study is made for the test house of BUILD to
map existing data on varying ventilation of roof constructions, but also to understand the
circumstances and the possible data to be drawn from the roof construction.
2.1
Known cases of withdrawn punched metal plate fasteners
The overall statements and points from St˚aende byggepanel [16] and registrations are
summarised within the present section, hence the experiences of withdrawn PMPF span
from beds in a garden to regular, standard roof constructions, and the location varies from
Denmark, England, Sweden, and Germany.
The problem of withdrawal of PMPF is large in Germany and England [16]. In Denmark,
the knowledge of problems with withdrawn PMPF is limited, which is clear based on the
discussion of the panel St˚aende byggepanel [16]. In recent years, 4 cases of W trusses with
withdrawn PMPF have been observed in Denmark [16]. 3 out of 4 cases are known in
detail and similarities in the conditions are observed. Also, a similar case in Sweden is
known more in detail. The extra case is included in the following observations since the
weather conditions are similar to the danish.
It is observed, that the 3+1 cases are alike thinking of: Minimum lifetime of 10 years,
markings on the timber indicate that the metal plate fasteners and the timber have been
in contact, the ventilation of the attics is inadequate, and the roof covering is dark [16].
The location of the PMPF that is withdrawn, is at the peak of the w truss or at the middle
of the upper flange, which in those cases were divided into two pieces. The question is
raised if it might be a weak location of the joint of the upper flange, hence at a place with
a relatively large moment that thereby affects the joint.
Further observations are done but are more individual [17]. At one building the PMPF has
only been withdrawn in the south- and west-facing facades. One building has indications
of large deformations of the upper truss. In one of the cases, there are similar buildings
located in the same area, but only the house which is built of low quality has the problem
of withdrawn PMPF [16].
Withdrawal of PMPF is not common, hence it is assumed that it might be related to
for example inadequate ventilation, dark roofing, and thereby movements of timber and
PMPF caused by temperature variation that might be larger than in standard attics [16].
Withdrawal of punched metal plate fasteners in trussed rafters
3
==PAGE:12==
The humidity- and temperature conditions in the cases are not established by systematic
measurements, but the timber is not assessed to have especially high or low moisture
content based on observations [16]. Additionally, the panel also discusses the opportunity
of static instability being a factor.
There was not an overview of the problem at the end of November 2022 [16], and no update
on the problem has been released since hence there are no indications of an overview in
Denmark. Research in the database of building inspection organizations (Huseftersynsor-
dningen) did not lead to any additional cases [16]. Further, a poll was made by contacting
suppliers, but no suppliers had information on this type of problem [16], which emphasizes
the size of the problem or at least the knowledge of the problem, thus it is not a widely
known problem.
In connection with this project, a registration in Fensmark, Denmark was performed. In
this case, PMPF were noted withdrawn in a terraced house by an occupant. Therefore,
registration of three similar terraced houses located next to each other was performed. At
the time of registration, some of the worst cases of withdrawn metal plate fasteners were
fixed, but all terraced houses had indications of the same problem regarding the withdrawal
of metal plate fasteners.
Thinking of the possible reasons for the problem worked out from the panel, markings in
the timber were clear, hence it is clear that the PMPF were originally close to the timber.
The locations of the problems are at the peak of the w trusses and at the joint of the
inclined compression truss to the upper flange. Generally for all houses, the PMPF in
these locations showed signs of withdrawal. The ventilation seemed to be fairly regular
since an opening at the heel of the roof as well as an opening close to the peak were clearly
free. Additionally, the moisture content of the timber was measured 10-12 %, which is
expected for timber in a well-ventilated attic. Therefore, the ventilation should not be
considered irregular in this case. Also, the static conditions seemed to be regular and
standard. Therefore, the reason for withdrawal in this case is unknown, but temperature
or RH might vary at specific times. Also, it could be considered if special loads in terms of
wind have happened to force the withdrawal, but then it is strange that it has happened to
three houses in a row. A full memorandum with sufficient picture documentation appears
in appendix B.
An additional registration was also performed, but this time in Virum. PMPF are used to
make beds in a garden in Virum, and after a couple of years, the MPF started to withdraw.
A full registration of the garden is performed of which a memorandum appears in appendix
C with picture documentation. Generally, most MPF are withdrawn from the timber 1-10
mm but with exceptions. The withdrawal of PMPF tends to happen more or faster on sides
exposed to thr sun, whereas shadow sides are more affected by algae and the withdrawal
4
Withdrawal of punched metal plate fasteners in trussed rafters
==PAGE:13==
is slower or almost non-existing. The worst withdrawn PMPF are at a side, which might
be one of the older parts of the beds, hence the time might be a stronger reason for the
large withdrawal than the location of the MPF.
The overall outcome of the registration is an indication of general trends, but there are
also exceptions. The reason for the exceptions is not clearly determined since there is a
lack of case-specific information and registrations over time. With that in mind, the results
of the registration only point out possible reasons. Thought, it confirms the possibility of
withdrawal of PMPF in specific conditions.
The MPF are affected by different conditions in the garden than in roof construction since
the joints in the garden are directly affected by the weather. Some joints are directly hit by
the sun, all joints are directly affected by water, and the temperature and RH might vary
faster or more extreme than in roof construction. Additionally, the joints in the garden
are loaded differently than typical joints in roof construction, since it is the soil pushing
perpendicular to the MPF in the garden, hence the load might force the withdrawal more
than the temperature and RH.
There are indications that direct contact with the sun can influence withdrawal, hence
high temperatures, which is possible in attics, might cause withdrawal. Furthermore, it
has been observed that the direction of PMPF may influence the withdrawal. However,
only one PMPF is rotated, so other reasons for the withdrawal might be significant.
The basis of the awareness of withdrawn PMPF is emphasized by the registration, hence
no conclusions can be drawn from the registration other than the actual conditions in
the specific case. Though, it confirms that extreme temperature and humidity conditions
and/or inappropriate loads are present at withdrawn PMPF.
2.2
Other tests
The mapping of other tests occurs within the present section, hence the learning is specif-
ically noted for the different tests. Tests regarding withdrawal of PMPF are limited and
at an early stage of development, whereas the test house has been used multiple times.
2.2.1
Withdrawal of punched metal plate fasteners
Finn Ehmsen has performed two small unscientific tests at the beginning of 2021. Tim-
ber joints connected by PMPF have been exposed to different humidity and temperature
conditions, while withdrawal of punched metal plate fasteners and moisture content of the
timber were continuously observed and measured. Both tests were interrupted after ap-
proximately one month since it was assessed that the timber was drying out, hence there
were no longer reasonable humidity conditions. A detailed description of the preliminary
Withdrawal of punched metal plate fasteners in trussed rafters
5
==PAGE:14==
unscientific experiment with the corresponding data and picture documentation occurs in
appendix A.
Experiences from these tests were considered as background in forming the tests within
the present project. Dried timber does not seem to cause the withdrawal in registered
roof constructions, since observations of RH and MC are at a standard level. Therefore,
it would be necessary for future tests to maintain reasonable conditions, hence keeping
the RH at a certain level while changing the temperature. On a more practical note, the
time frame of these tests is the starting point for estimating the time frame of forcing
withdrawal, hence it is more than a month or 22 repetitions. Though, the withdrawal in
this experiment might be caused by drying, so the predicted time frame is uncertain. The
most important point is that the experiment verifies the possibility to force a withdrawal
of PMPF to happen.
2.2.2
Temperature, moisture, and ventilation in roof constructions
Tests regarding ventilation have been performed in the test house of BUILD. The purpose
of the tests is more related to materials in ceiling constructions and vapor barriers, but an
overview of the properties and corresponding possibilities with the test house are clearly
stated based on the reports.
The first study is by Thor Hansen [11], and in continuation of the Ph.D.-project, a master
project has been performed in the test house with the title: ”Ventilations influence on
hygrothermal conditions in ventilated roof space” [12], which is the main source of the test
house conditions and properties since it is the newest known use of the test house. The main
objective of the master project is to compare 1) the relationship between the hygrothermal
conditions and air change and 2) the relationship between hygrothermal conditions and
vapor barrier, insulation material, and insulation thickness. Though, the circumstances
of the object allow indications of the relationship between temperature, relative humidity,
and ventilation to occur. Additionally, information on using the test house is of a high
value.
The test spanned over 3 months from January to March 2019, which were assessed a bit
too short to make conclusions about the use of vapor barrier.
Further, all results are
only based on the three-month period. Looking further into the results, only minimal
differences in RH are seen across different types of ceiling construction, and they might
be caused by measurement uncertainties of the equipment. Hence, the parameter with the
greatest significance in the specific section and subsection cannot be stated with certainty.
Generally, the measurements indicate a greater influence of ventilation rate than insulation
thickness thinking of the relative humidity in the attic. This indicates that the moisture
content in the attic primarily comes from the ventilation and less from the diffusion and
convection. Also, it indicates that the air change has a great influence on the moisture
6
Withdrawal of punched metal plate fasteners in trussed rafters
==PAGE:15==
content in the attic. Though, the report points out that many parameters influence the
conditions in the attic. For example, insolation from the sun heats up the attic so the
temperature rise, which cause a dip in the RH. Additionally, the measurements show that
reduction of air change, in general, rises the RH.
Regulations of the air change have been done in two out of three sections with six different
variations of ceiling construction. They aimed for a reduction of 1/3 and 2/3, and then
the last section was used as a reference. The ventilation within the project ended up being
reduced by 0%, 35%, and 51% in relation to the recommended ventilation. The ratios of the
reduced ventilation are verified by measurements using a tracer gas according to the PFT
method. The information on reduced ventilation maps the expected current conditions in
the test house since it is the latest known documented change in ventilation.
The method to reduce the ventilation has been clearly described, hence a simple method
has been used, namely plugging out some of the ventilation area with foam by hand. It
has been done for respectively the heel and peak ventilation of the roof. The ventilation
conditions seemed to be stable in the three months the test lasted, hence the material and
method used to close the holes should be suitable and durable for this type of test.
Withdrawal of punched metal plate fasteners in trussed rafters
7
==PAGE:16==
3
Behavior of wood and steel
The timber joints consist of timber connected by punched metal plate fasteners, PMPF.
The properties of respectively wood and steel are therefore explained and elaborated with
especially temperature and humidity in mind to clear the possible reactions of the materials
under certain conditions.
3.1
Material properties of wood
Wood is a material that is affected by the surrounding conditions in terms of temperature
and relative humidity. The properties of the material vary in different fiber directions, with
different temperatures, and changing moisture content, which is elaborated in the present
sections.
3.1.1
Structure
The main structure of a tree consists of roots that anchor the tree, a trunk that carries
the crown and transports water and nutrients, and a crown that consists of branches with
leaves, which transform water and chlorophyll into nutrients. It is from the trunk that
timber is produced [1], hence it consists of wood cells. The growth period of trees starts
at the sprouting of the seed, where the shoot grows and closes at the end of the growth
period with an end or side bud. Buds start to appear during the growth period of the tree
[1]. As the tree grows a regular trunk occurs. It consists of a middle, pitch, going radially
out to the heartwood, and sapwood, and ends at the bark. Concurrently, cells grow from
the pitch to the bark to carry nutrients. These circumstances in the growth period shape
the material properties of the carved wood in terms of for example strength, which differs
depending on the direction. Therefore, wood is an orthotropic material. Additionally, the
density is depending on the growth of the tree as well as knots and other irregularities of
the structure. The timber consists of wood cells that have been developed through the
growth period of the tree. These cells are mainly elongated and occur in the longitudinal
direction [6].
3.1.2
Moisture content
Wood is a porous, hygroscopic material meaning that the material is able of binding water
molecules from the air [9]. It contains water partly in the lumen of the cell and partly bound
to the cell walls [6]. Moisture content, MC, describes the amount of water in a product,
hence within this report, it represents the percentage ratio between the weight of water in
the timber and the dry timber. The MC of timber effect for example density, dimensions,
strength, deformations, and thermal conductivity significant [9]. The calculation of MC, u,
appears in equation 1 and depends on the masses [g] of water and dry timber, respectively.
Also, the MC can be described with the volume by multiplying u with the dry density.
8
Withdrawal of punched metal plate fasteners in trussed rafters
==PAGE:17==
u =
mwater
mdry timber
· 100%
(1)
In general practice it is easier to only measure the piece of timber, hence equation 1 can
be transformed to equation 2 only including masses of the timber. The mass of water can
be calculated with the dry-weight-method by having the wet mass of one piece of wood,
dry the wood piece at 103 ℃± 2 ℃[9] or 105 ℃[6], and then measure the dry weight of
the wood piece. Then the calculation occur to be equation 2 with m = masses [g] and u
= MC [%].
u = mwet timber −mdry timber
mdry timber
· 100%
(2)
In the present project, the workpieces have not been dried out, hence the dry timber
mass is calculated from a known MC and wet timber mass. Therefore, equation 2 can be
transformed to equation 3 for calculation of the dry timber mass with m = masses [g] and
u = MC [%].
mdry timber = mwet timber ·
100%
u + 100%
(3)
Additionally, MC can be specified faster but not as accurately [9] by using the electronic
properties of moisture. The indirect method entails measurements between two electrodes
which are inserted into the timber at the wanted location. At that location, the MC can
be specified in the interval of 2-26 % with the accuracy of 1-2% with the best gauges.
Wood is a dielectric material and resistance, which the electric equipment take advantage
of. Resistance-based is practical in use on construction sites. Though, the resistance-based
gauge can only be accurate under fiber saturation point, whereas the dielectric based
includes all stages of MC.
Equilibrium moisture content, EMC, is the condition, where the amount of water in the
material is in balance with the water in the air, hence there is no measurable exchange of
water molecules between material and air. Thereby, the weight of for example wood pieces
will be stable when EMC is reached. EMC depends on the temperature and RH of the air,
which occurs in equation 4, hence EMC can be calculated when the temperature and RH
are known [10].
EMC = 1800

(4)

parameters are calculated as W = 349 + 1.29 · T + 1.35 · 10−2 · T 2, K = 0.805 + 7.36 ·

1.91 + 4.07 · 10−2 · T −2.93 · 10−4 · T 2.
Fiber saturation point, FSP, is when all water is removed from the lumen of the cell while
the cell wall is still saturated. The FSP is typically 25-35 % but depends on the type of
wood. FSP for ordinary timber calculations in general practice can 30 % be used. Further,
the drying-up happens by water released from the cell walls and humidity released from the
cell lumen. FSP has a great influence on the practical application, since the moisture level
lower than FSP for many types of wood affects the physical and mechanical properties,
whereas most properties are independent of water content above FSP [9].
Hygroscopic materials interact with the surrounding climate, hence the MC varies with the
relative humidity. Relative humidity, RH, is the ratio of actual water vapor content and
the capacity of water vapor content at a specific temperature. 100% RH is the saturated
vapor pressure, which will be elaborated in section 4. The amount of water in the air is
calculated from known saturated vapor pressure with equation 5 from which figure 1 occur
[3]. It appears that the capacity of water vapor content is affected by the temperature,
hence if the amount of water vapor content is stable, a temperature change can change
the RH. Further, when the temperature increases the air can contain larger amounts of
moisture, hence the capacity of water vapor content increase with the temperature.
v =
p
461.4 · T
(5)
Figure 1: Ratio of temperature and water vapor content
10
Withdrawal of punched metal plate fasteners in trussed rafters
==PAGE:19==
The relative humidity and temperature vary around the year with the seasons in Denmark,
why the MC of wood varies around the year. Typically in indoor conditions, the MC range
from 12% to 14% in the summer, whereas it ranges from 7% to 10% in the winter. In the
outdoor conditions, the MC is around 14% in the summer. In the winter the MC depends
on the protection against the weather [6]. A MC of 18%-25% is typically reachable for
wood exposed to the weather, whereas protected wood in for example roof constructions
typically can reach a maximum MC of 16%-20% [6]. Though, the exact MC still depends
on the weather hence the temperature and RH of the air surrounding the wood are the
affecting parameters of the MC.
The curves in figure 1 are mean curves. Therefore, an elaboration with more details about
multiple iterations of adsorption and desorption can be found in figure 2 at page 12. The
long small dotted line with an arrow pointing down is the first desorption. Afterwards,
the first adsorption occur as the solid line with an arrow up. It do not reach the starting
level. Therefore, the second desorption occur a bit below the first desorption as the dashed
line with an arrow pointing down. Then the hysteresis loop occur. Accordingly, the EMC
decrease a bit after the first loop at the same RH. Desorption is decreasing EMC and RH,
while absorption is increasing MC and RH. The outcome of the curve’s progress is a lower
MC at the same RH as the start of the first desorption in the loop. Sorption hysteresis is
typical for wood [9]. The timber in the tests which is exposed to varying RH will also be
exposed to moistening and drying, hence the MC of the timber follows a hysteresis loop
like in figure 2, when it is exposed to multiple cycles of variation [2].
Withdrawal of punched metal plate fasteners in trussed rafters
11
==PAGE:20==
Figure 2: Outline of hysteresis loop [2]
Binding to primary places of sorption should happen approximately up to 90% RH. Above
approximately 90%, the capillary condensation of eventually micro pore starts in combi-
nation with aggregate of water molecules around the ions in the cell wall [9]. The relation
between swelling of the volume and water absorption decrease above 90% RH even when
RH increase, since capillary condensation do not increase the volume of the wood [9].
3.1.3
Thermal characteristics
Specific heat capacity, thermal conductivity, thermal expansion, and thereby the ther-
mal diffusivity are all properties that can characterize the differences of wood from other
materials in changing temperature and humidity conditions. A combination of thermal
conductivity, λ, density, ρ, and specific heat capacity, cp, describes how fast the wood
absorb heat from its surroundings and thereby how fast the temperature change, hence
the thermal diffusivity, a, can be calculated by a = λ/ρ · cp.[9]. The thermal diffusivity of
wood is typically lower than other materials.
12
Withdrawal of punched metal plate fasteners in trussed rafters
==PAGE:21==
Specific heat capacity is the needed energy for increasing one kg. wood one degree hence
a unit of [kJ/kg−1K−1]. Wood that contains water has a greater heat capacity than dried
wood [10]. Specific heat capacity is higher for wood than for metals like steel, since it is
1.65 [kJ/kg−1K−1] for fir and 0.50 [kJ/kg−1K−1] for steel [5].
The heat flow through a thickness of a material exposed to a temperature difference is the
thermal conductivity. The thermal conductivity of structural wood is a bit larger than
insulation materials but low compared to steel and is also depending on the direction of
fibers caused by the anisotropic structure of the material [9]. The size range of thermal
conductivity could for example be structural softwood with 12% MC, which has a range
of 0.10 to 0.14 Wm−1K−1, and steel with thermal conductivity of 45 Wm−1K−1 [10]. The
thermal conductivity increases when the MC increase. The conductivity in the longitudinal
direction is approximate twice the size of the transverse direction [9]. It is proportional
to density and positively correlated to MC and temperature. Additionally, it is influenced
by, for example, grain direction and structural irregularities such as checks and knots. The
temperature affects the thermal conductivity by approximately 2% to 3% per 10 ℃hence
a relatively small impact [10].
Thermal expansion of wood takes place with increasing temperatures, hence the dimensions
change relatively with temperature change. The coefficient of the longitudinal direction
is 4 · 10−6K−1, and 40 · 10−6K−1 in the transverse direction, hence a difference of 10 in
size with the transverse direction expanding most. Though, the thermal expansion is often
exceeded by the moisture-related change in dimensions, since a change in temperature and
change in humidity often goes together [9]. First, the moisture in the wood expands with
the temperature, hence the wood will expand. Further, the expansion will decrease when
the moisture slowly leave the wood. In the moisture level of 8% to 20%, hence the level
relevant within the present project, this phenomena will be dominating rather than the
thermal expansion. For wood at these usual moisture levels, the end expansion might be
negative after prolonged heating.
3.2
Material properties of steel
Steel is a metal, hence crystalline material, which is characterized by specific, spatially,
and regularly arrangement of atoms. Though, in natural crystals small defects occur in
the systems, which affect the properties of the metal in terms of strength and deformations.
Hence, steel behave as an isotropic material since the mechanical properties in terms of
strength and stiffness even though the micro structure is inhomogeneous. Steel is not a
porous material but compact, hence water from the air is not absorbed by the material.
Instead, the water settles as dew at the surface if the right conditions occur.
The thermal characteristics of steel are mentioned in relation to the wood in section 3.1,
hence the specific heat capacity for steel is lower than for wood, since it is 1.65 [kJ/kg−1K−1]
Withdrawal of punched metal plate fasteners in trussed rafters
13
==PAGE:22==
for fir and 0.50 [kJ/kg−1K−1] for steel [5]. Thermal expansion of steel occur when the
temperature increase. The thermal diffusivity of steel is higher than wood, which is caused
by the high thermal conductivity and moderate density and specific heat capacity of the
steel [10]. Typically, the wood would be 1.6 · 10−7m2s−1 while steel would be 1 · 10−5m2s−1
[10]. The thermal conductivity of steel is relatively high hence 45 Wm−1K−1 [10], and the
same in all directions opposite wood.
The thermal expansion of steel happens with increasing temperature as it happens for
wood.
Again, steel has the same value in all directions opposite wood.
The thermal
expansion for steel is 12.1·10−6K−1 [5]. With a temperature change of 40℃it corresponds
to 0.48 mm/m, whereas the wood would be 0.16 mm/m and 1.6 mm/m for respectively
the longitudinal and transverse directions.
14
Withdrawal of punched metal plate fasteners in trussed rafters
==PAGE:23==
4
Roof structures
Punched metal plate fasteners, PMPF, are used in connecting timber roof structures.
Therefore, a brief introduction of roofs and attics occurs as well as an elaboration of
timber joints and the conditions in the attics occur.
The shape and types of roofs are many of which examples could be gable roofs, clipped
gable roofs, and hip roofs. Thinking of the roof slope, roofs are divided into two groups:
Pitched roofs and flat roofs [4].
The choice and thereby shape of the roof affect the
structural system in terms of roof trusses. Types of roof trusses are for example w-truss,
attic truss, and scissor truss, but the overall idea is to carry the self-load, snow-load, and
wind-load efficiently in terms of use and load carrying capacity. The trusses are mostly
frames that are stabilized and connected during the building phase of the roof. Timber roof
trusses are optimized structures, hence a low material consumption and a great strength,
which cause the importance of following the correct construction and bracing of the rafter
[8]. The material used in Denmark is typically softwood in terms of fir. The structural
systems allow systematical access to produce the frames. They can be connected at the
construction site but can also be produced on a fabric. Additionally, the construction of the
roof is affecting the use of the building and the attic [4]. Attics can be both unventilated
and ventilated. Typically, roofs are built with ventilated attics [4]. An attic is defined as
ventilated when a roof cavity occurs between the ceiling and the external roof occur.
4.1
Timber joints
Wood elements in roof construction can be connected with multiple different materials
and methods depending on the location and function of the joint especially thinking of
the forces that should be obtained and transferred through the joint. For example bonded
gusset plate, bolts, bolts with inlaid connectors, bolts with pressed-in connectors, nails,
and PMPF [7]. Glue is the stiffest connector, whereas bolts with inlaid connectors are
the most compliant [7].
The load carrying capacity per area varies from 0.5-5.0 MPa
depending on the load type. Bolts have the lowest capacity per area, while PMPF has
the highest [7]. Joints with PMPF are dominating in the production of light trusses and
single-family houses [7], since the conditions are uniform and predictable, and the roofs
are simple. Therefore, the procedure of calculations and production can be standardized
at the fabrics. It contains specific equipment to push the metal plates uniform into the
rafters, hence the metal plates are mainly used in special factories [7], where the necessary
conditions can be obtained to secure the joints to reach the stated load carrying capacity.
The plate should be pushed and fastened in the wood all at once [7], so barbs of the nails
bind to the timber. The load carrying capacity depends for example on the shape, size, and
location of nails, the plate thickness, and the type of steel [7]. The metal plate fasteners
used within this present project are chosen based on the most commonly used in Palsgaard
Spær, which is the largest truss factory in Denmark [15]. The types are GNT100S-small,
GNT100S-large, and MiTopW, which are explained in section 5.2.1.
Withdrawal of punched metal plate fasteners in trussed rafters
15
==PAGE:24==
4.2
Ventilation
Roofs are exposed to moisture in terms of precipitation, water vapor from outside, water
vapor from inside the house through diffusion in the materials or convection through small
leakage, and water from the construction phase [4]. Moisture exposure can also occur if the
wind hit the roof so precipitation appears going upwards, hence in unfortunate situations,
the water can get through for example connections with overlap [4]. To avoid the small
accumulation of water that inevitably penetrates, the roofs are traditionally ventilated.
The ventilation can be secured by vents in the peak and bottom of the roofs [4]. Further,
effective ventilation requires general guidelines concerning among others the area of the
vents, even distribution of the ventilation air, and adjustments if nets are used to avoid
invading insects. In periods, the construction will be exposed to moisture from the air for
example if the trusses are colder than the dew point, but this will settle as the relative
humidity, RH, decrease.
The roof is well-ventilated when the vapor pressure in the attic is similar to the outside
vapor pressure. The vapor pressure is based on the saturated vapor pressure and is affected
by the RH. Saturated vapor pressure, pm, is calculated with equation 6 where θ is the
temperature [℃] and the result occur in [Pa]. The formula is not exact, but the deviation
is less than 0.15% in the present temperature area [3].
pm = 610.5 · e
17.269·θ
237.3+θ for θ ≥0◦C
(6)
At the saturated vapor pressure, the RH is 100% as explained in section 3.1. The relation
to the vapor pressure at different RH is simple as it is calculated by a multiplication of the
saturated vapor pressure with the respective RH. Selected RH are plotted at the curve in
figure 3.
Figure 3: Ratio of temperature and vapor pressure
16
Withdrawal of punched metal plate fasteners in trussed rafters
==PAGE:25==
The temperature in the ventilated attics is affected by the temperature outside, but it is
also heated by the sun. A reference year of conditions outside occur in figure 4 [3]. The
RH is largest in the winter, while it decreases in the spring to reach the lowest extreme in
the summer. On the other hand, the temperature is lowest in the winter and increases to a
peak in the summer. Therefore, large changes in temperature and RH occur in the month
of interest within this project. Measurements at the test house have previously shown that
the temperature is larger and the RH is lower than the reference year, but the tendency of
development is the same [12].
Figure 4: TRY weather conditions of reference year [3]
The average of hours of sun per day in March, April, and May 2022 in Copenhagen are
respectively, 7.37 h, 8.40 h, and 6.82 h [13]. With the data of the weather conditions in
Denmark, and thinking of the many hours of sun per day, the conditions in an attic at this
period are expected to reach way higher temperatures than the outside conditions.

5
Method
The tests in the present project consist of test 1 which is performed in the test house at
BUILD and tests 2 and 3 which are performed at DTU. The test house at BUILD has been
used before, hence the house is standing, measuring equipment is installed, and a system of
reading the measured data is made. Therefore, the method consists of understanding the
setup from others described in section 2.2. Additionally, the handling of data is developed
and formed to reach the scope.
Tests 2 and 3 are at DTU and are performed in climate chambers. The tests investigate
joints exposed to respective variations of temperature and variation of relative humidity,
RH, and their reaction to the changing conditions, especially if a withdrawal of punched
metal plate fasteners, PMPF, occurs. The execution of the tests is based on the methods
described in section 2.2, hence it only relies on a small unscientific experiment and gen-
eral experience with the withdrawal of PMPF. Additionally, the tests are developed and
adjusted to the available facilities at DTU. Therefore, the process of performing the tests
has been growing and changing direction based on knowledge of the climate chambers.
5.1
Test 1: Test house
The test at BUILD consists of a reduction of the ventilation at the test house. Measure-
ments of RH, temperature, and ventilation at different points of the roof structure and
outside are performed automatically, while MC of the timber trusses have been measured
manually. The focus is mainly on the relation between these conditions, but the test house
is also set up with different materials in the roof construction, which will be explained and
considered.
5.1.1
Test facilities
The test house at BUILD is located in Copenhagen, Denmark at a parking lot as it occurs
in figure 5 at page 19. The test house is a light structure with w-trusses as the roof, and
built after building standards, hence only the materials in the roof differ. The general
information of the test house is based on the Master these ”Ventilation’s influence on
hygrothermal conditions in ventilated roof space” [12] and supported by observations before
the test has been started.

5.1.3
Moving average
The data from the test house consists of a variety of temperatures and RH over a day. It
should be comparable with the MC of the trusses in the attic, so the analysis is durable.
Therefore, a need of processing the data emerge. Moving average, MA, is a widely used
method of smoothing fluctuations. The idea is to divide the original series of data into
multiple subseries of which an average is made, and a new series of averages occur [14].
The variation of temperature and RH smooth out by creating averages, hence exceptional
extreme days only influence the average, but do not influence the analysis concrete. Dif-
ferent methods are investigating processing the data from the test house. Both a simple
moving average and an exponential moving average are used.
The method of MA in Excel is a simple moving average, SMA. Here, the calculation is
based on the average time in the subseries. Within the present project time, t, is hours or
days. SMA is calculated with the equation 7. A is the original value of the point, Pt is
the new value at t point, and n is the chosen number of time values in the subseries. P
is an average of the number of hours or days in the past, so the new series of data start
staggered from the start, hence it is staggered n hours or days.
Pt = At + At−1 + ... + At−n+1
n
(7)
Exponential moving average, EMA, allows a greater influence on recent temperature and
RH since it is a weighted average.
This method starts at point one as it occurs from
equation 8. Afterward, equation 9 is valid, hence the percentages can vary, but within
the present project, a ratio of 10% vs. 90% is chosen. Development of the use of moving
average occurs as a part of the results and analysis in section 6.1.1.
M(1) = X(1)
(8)
M(i) = 90% · M(i −1) + 10% · X(i) for i > 1
(9)
5.2
Test 2 and 3: Climate chambers
The tests at DTU consist of timber joints exposed to varying RH or temperatures. 36
workpieces of timber joints are handled the same way before the tests, hence they are
described before the two tests. The workpieces are tested in a small and a large climate
chamber precisely with varying RH and temperature, respectively. The climate chambers
have different setups, which are explained in the present section. Additionally, the tested
workpieces needed apparatus, and the test setup will be presented in the present section
with the corresponding considerations and reason for the choices.

5.2.1
Workpieces
The selected workpieces are systematically produced in terms of the type of joint, type of
PMPF, and wood density. Three types of joints have been produced to map both joints
with parallel fibers of the timber, joints with perpendicular fibers of the timber, and joints
with fibers in an extension of each other. Additionally, three types of PMPF have been
chosen as well as the density has been divided into two categories: Low and high. This
combination led to 18 needed workpieces and to have two sets 36 workpieces have been
produced.
A brief overview of the workpieces is at table 1.
In the table, the type of
workpieces are called T-, P- and L-joint to differ between T-joint, parallel joint, and length
joint. L and H refer to respectively low and high in the type of wood, while the ’x’ differ
between two batches of wood. All workpieces are C24 since it is a commonly used material
for roof constructions.
The production of workpieces has performed like the normal production, hence some of the
workpieces have knots or cracks with resin under the PMPF, close to the PMPF, or not
at all on the workpiece. Also, the punching of the metal plate fasteners is approximately
properly, but on some of the workpieces there are small gaps between the plate and wood,
or the PMPF are punched a bit too much in the wood. The PMPF are in some cases not
precisely placed so the distance to the edges varies a bit, hence the PMPF are not exactly
parallel or perpendicular to the grains.
These small differences and irregularities have
happened randomly in the production, which is a common variation in the manufacturing
of products, hence the load bearing capacities are still obtained for the joints. All specific
data for each workpiece are noted and occur in appendix C in table form, while pictures
of all workpieces are in appendix E. An elaborated description of the types of joints and
PMPF occurs in the following sections as well as the preparation of the workpieces before
the tests in terms of MC and division of the workpieces.

5.2.1.1
Type of joint
Three types of joints with PMPF are chosen within the present project. The three types
are selected thinking of the direction of the grains on the two pieces of timber connected,
and on the direction of the nails on the metal plate fastener. The joints are of type: T-,
parallel- and length-joints, where the grains of the two elements of timber are respectively
parallel, perpendicular and in continuation of each other. The three types of joints are
shown in figure 14.
(a) T-joint
(b) Parallel joint
(c) Length joint
Figure 14: Types of joints
5.2.1.2
Type of punched metal plate fastener
In figure 15 and 16 at page 28 an overview of the three different punched metal plate
fasteners appears, hence the different nails and corresponding amount of nails of the metal
plate fasteners occur. The GNT100S-plates have the same type of nails but differ in the
number of nails and size of the plate. The MiTopW is a relatively new developed metal
plate fastener that Palsgaard Spær has taken into their production, and both the shape
and size of nails vary from GNT100S. Also, there are two different types of nails on that
metal plate fastener - one with two legs and one with one leg.
(a) GNT100S-small
(b) GNT100S-large

5.2.1.3
Moisture content - preparation of workpieces
Before testing the workpieces, they were stored in a climate room to get in moisture
equilibrium for approximately 2 months. The conditions in the climate room were 20℃
and MC of 65% of which the specifications occur in section 5.2.2.1. The weight of the
workpieces was measured over time since the weight indicates if the workpieces are in
balance with the RH, hence 4 workpieces were selected to be followed. The Equilibrium
moisture content was accepted when 4 high-density elements had a weight difference of less
than 0.1 g. per day, hence the curve was assessed as flattened out. The weights appear in
figure 17 at page 29 from which it occurs that the curves did flatten out.
28
Withdrawal of punched metal plate fasteners in trussed rafters
==PAGE:37==
Figure 17: Weight of 4 workpieces before the tests
Additionally, the MC have been measured to approximately 9.2-10.5% with the equipment
shown in figure 18. The measuring equipment is calibrated manually at the beginning of
the measuring after inserting the electric sensors, which occur in figure 18b. Then the MC
can be read. Afterward, the MC is corrected by the type of wood hence fir. The results
indicate that the conditions in the climate room might vary a bit from the conditions in
the setup since the MC was expected to be closer to the EMC of 12% but measuring
uncertainties might also influence.
(a) Setup
(b) Close up
Figure 18: Measuring equipment for moisture content
Wood elements tend to dry out and absorb moisture faster from the ends than from the
Withdrawal of punched metal plate fasteners in trussed rafters
29
==PAGE:38==
sides, hence the risk of deformations is larger than the conditions in real roof construction.
This is caused by the fact that the length of trusses connected by metal plate fasteners
in roof constructions normally is longer, hence the deformation of the timber close to the
PMPF might not be as large as the small elements of timber within this project. Therefore,
the PMPF might be affected differently than reality. Additionally, this phenomenon might
lead to cracks in the ends of the wood elements. A possible solution to solving this problem
would be to seal the ends of the workpieces with wax or varnish, but that might also slow
the process down, which could lead to no reactions within the time frame of the project.
Therefore, the timber has not been sealed.

5.2.3
Test 2: Joints affected by varying temperature with constant relative humidity
Test 2 is set up to investigate whether withdrawal of PMPF can occur with changing
temperature in the interval 20-60℃. The hypothesis is that the heat will expand the steel,
which might force a withdrawal of the PMPF. The steel has a large surface in direct contact
with the air in relation to the total amount of steel, hence it is expected to expand faster
than the wood also thinking of the thermal characteristics. The wood will expand as well,
but the heat might not transfer quickly into the middle of the workpiece, hence the large
volume related to the surface might delay the expansion. Combined with the fact, that
expansion in the longitudinal direction is miner compared to steel, it seems reasonable that
the expansion of respectively steel and wood might work against each other. Thinking of
the weather conditions in Denmark and many hours of sun per day, the temperature in an
attic is expected to reach way higher temperatures than the outside conditions, hence 60℃
is assessed as a reasonable extreme. When the temperature increase, the EMC increase as
well, which appear in figure 20. Though, the change is relatively small at 65% RH, which
occurs in test 2. Therefore, the workpieces will primarily be affected by the temperature
change. The test facilities, equipment, workpieces, setup, and performance of the test are
presented in the following section with the corresponding considerations.
Figure 20: EMC at different temperatures
32
Withdrawal of punched metal plate fasteners in trussed rafters
==PAGE:41==

5.2.3.2
Cycles and calibration of climate chamber
In test 2 the temperature varies. The temperature change of the workpieces’ surfaces is
assessed relatively fast, hence the large climate chamber is set up to do a program with
multiple cycles of varying temperatures and stable RH in a day. A step-by-step guide on
how to use the large climate chamber occurs in appendix F, but the overall method and
corresponding intention are explained in the following section.
The method in this test is based on the cases of withdrawal of PMPF, which are discovered
many years after the construction, hence the goal is mainly to accomplish as many cycles
as possible within the time frame.
The strategy is to test the capacity of the climate
chamber, so the cycles get calibrated with the capacity, and the conditions thereby follow
the programmed cycles.
Additionally, the intention is to evaluate the accuracy of the
internal measuring equipment based on the external measuring equipment. The starting
point occurs in table 9. The extreme temperatures of 20 ℃and 60 ℃are assessed to
be reasonable based on possible temperatures in attics and sufficient for contraction and
expansion of the materials to happen.
Table 9: Starting point of cycles intervals
Constant
Ramping
Constant
Ramping
Temperature [C]
20
20-60
60
60-20
Time [min]
45
15
45
15
Hence, one cycle of 2 hours would be as illustrated in figure 23. The first step is constant
at 20℃marked with orange, ramping happens over 15 minutes in the next purple step, the
blue step is constant at 60℃, and lastly, ramping occurs in the yellow step for 15 minutes.
Figure 23: System of one cycle
Withdrawal of punched metal plate fasteners in trussed rafters
35
==PAGE:44==
The repetition of cycles in a day occurs in figure 24, accordingly 12 cycles in a day with
the given intervals.
Figure 24: System of 12 cycles in one day
The climate chamber has been programmed to perform the 12 cycles without workpieces in
it and was started with random temperature and RH. The external measuring equipment
was not relocated, hence it hangs close to the wall of the climate chamber approximately
halfway up. It is clear from figure 25 that the conditions in the climate chamber deviate
from the presets. The temperature does not reach a stable plateau either at the top or
bottom. Similarly, the RH does not manage to get stable, which is caused by the continu-
ously changing temperature. The random values at the starting point do not influence the
cycles since they are so close to the values of the cycle. The same development applies to
the noted observations for the internal measuring equipment, hence the temperature does
not follow the programmed temperature, why the conditions do not reach a stable point.
Though, the RH seems more stable for the sensors of the climate chamber.
Figure 25: 12 cycles per day, external measuring equipment
The values of the external sensors seem to be a bit off, hence the temperature varies from
15.6℃to 56.4 ℃, and the RH varies from 41.6% to 83.8%. The temperature varies 40.8℃,
36
Withdrawal of punched metal plate fasteners in trussed rafters
==PAGE:45==
which is close to the expected value of 40 ℃, hence a lack of calibration might cause
the difference between respectively the lowest and the highest temperature. Otherwise,
the external sensor might be affected more by the wall of the climate chamber than the
air, hence the location of the external sensors is investigated more in-depth. The specific
fluctuation peaks define the variation of RH, but the main problem is the fact, that the
development over time does not get stable. The RH is expected to get peaks caused by a
change in temperature, but it should stabilize.
Overall, it appeared from the pretest that the climate chamber did not achieve the tem-
perature of 60℃within the 15 minutes, hence the step of constant temperature ended up
varying. As a result of the steep ramping, the climate chamber prioritized reaching the
temperature, which affected the relative humidity to fluctuate more than requested. As a
result of these observations, changes have been made. The external sensors have been relo-
cated with more space to the wall. The extreme temperatures are prioritized to maintain,
why the time of intervals has been reconsidered with the outcome seen in table 10.

5.2.4
Test 3: Joints affected by varying relative humidity with constant temperature
Test 3 consists of workpieces affected by varying RH since it is suspected to cause the
withdrawal of PMPF in timber. The RH outside varies as well as the RH varies with the
temperature. Extremes of RH are chosen to 20% and 85% based on outside conditions.
The temperature in the attic forces the highest RH a bit down in the winter, where the
42
Withdrawal of punched metal plate fasteners in trussed rafters
==PAGE:51==
temperatures are expected to be a bit higher than outside, but the temperature also forces
the lowest RH a bit down in the summer period again caused by the relatively higher
temperature. The variation of RH will cause variation of EMC, which occurs in figure 32.
The starting point is an EMC of 12.0%, the lowest EMC is 9.3%, and the highest EMC is
18% if the workpieces obtain the capacity. This theoretical values are used as the basis of
the test even though the MC have been measured a bit smaller.
Figure 32: EMC at different relative humidity
The used equipment and laboratory test facilities are described with the corresponding con-
siderations using them. Further, the practical setup is described including the workpieces
and the performance of the test.

manufacturer Weiss Technik Nederland B.V., and the model number is EKE.70.180.110.M.
It is located at the structural lab at the Technical University of Denmark in Lyngby, Den-
mark, and it occurs in figure 33 at page 44. The inner dimensions of the chamber are
approximately 33 cm in width, 84 cm in height, and 50 cm in depth.

The setup consists of a small chamber on top of the supplier, but except for the water
supply - a white plastic dunk - that is placed at the top of the small chamber. The water
supply is operated manually. The lamps are installed in the chamber, hence they can resist
changes in RH and temperature. They can be turned on at the setup menu shown in figure
34 at page 46 from which the main menu also occurs in terms of the two black screens with
green numbers. The large numbers at the top are the conditions in the chamber, while
the numbers at the bottom are the set points. The small square at the top indicates if
the machine actively works on changing the values. For example, the green square at the
right part of ’Climate’ is turned on, hence RH is decreasing. If it was increasing the green
square would be more to the left. It turns on in steps, why the square at ’Temp.’ is not
turned on at the moment of taking the picture.
Withdrawal of punched metal plate fasteners in trussed rafters
45
==PAGE:54==
Figure 34: Set up menu at the small climate chamber
The change of set point is reached by clicking on the right circular button to break the
’run’, hence ’hold’ turns green. It is possible to push the arrows up and down, while ’hold’
is green. The two squared buttons to the left bottom are used if the setup of the chamber
should be changed.
Before starting the test, the small climate chamber is tested for being stable over time.
The temperature can be stabilized at a constant value in the small climate chamber.
Concurrently, the RH can be changed only with small variations and deviations at the
modification phase. This procedure of changing the RH is done manually since the small
climate chamber is not set up for programming even though the machine is capable of being
automatized. The change is done in one step, where the set point steps instantaneously
from its current value to the new end set point. The chamber is able to keep relatively
stable and predictable conditions since the chamber is relatively small. The manual work
allows the time of modification to be assessed during the test based on the weight of the
46
Withdrawal of punched metal plate fasteners in trussed rafters
==PAGE:55==
workpieces. No influence of heat from lamps are observed, hence they can be turned on
as required. These conclusions and the following plan of the method are made based on
testing the chamber before the workpieces are placed in the chamber.
The small climate chamber is assessed to accommodate three workpieces. Then, the airflow
in the climate chamber should be feasible, hence a stable temperature and RH should
be possible to reach.
This is confirmed by the observations during the test where the
RH changes in approximately the same pace as before inserting the workpiece into the
chamber and the chamber keeps the conditions stable. The chamber only overshoots a
little bit as the temperature maximal gets ± 0.2 ℃and ± 0.4 % RH in the modification
phase. Further, the chamber seems to prioritize a stable temperature at the modification
phase.
Measurements of the conditions in terms of temperature and RH in the climate chamber
are done by observations at the changing point. Additionally, the chamber has been verified
to be stable over time, hence no need for external measurements was assessed needed for
the test to be performed properly. Also, experiences from test 2 were considered as a
breakdown of the measuring equipment occurred.
The opening of the chamber for weighing has been tested to assess the influence since it
got too time-consuming to set up the weights in the chamber with a measuring station
connected. When the chamber gets opened the temperature and MC are affected by the
conditions in the surroundings of the chamber. The temperature was mostly close to each
other, while the MC in the surroundings was closer to 50% than 85%, hence the conditions
in the chamber were primarily influenced by the opening when the RH was high. Though,
the small climate chamber does get stable relatively fast.
5.2.4.2
Workpieces and apparatus
The workpieces have been stabilized in terms of MC of which a detailed description occurs
in section 5.2.1.3.
The method for choosing workpieces is explained in section 5.2.1.4,
but the dimension of the workpiece limits the possible amount to be 3 pieces. Therefore,
workpieces with the same type of PMPF are chosen, hence differences or similarities of fiber
direction connected to a specific type of PMPF are possible. Further, the weight class of
the workpieces is the same. Accordingly, the only general difference of the workpieces is
the type of joint: T-, parallel-, and length-joint, as the specifically selected workpieces: 6,
18, and 33, respectively.
The workpieces are weighted before, during, and at the end of the test. The used scale are
of quality with the smallest standard displayed division of d = 0.1 g. The possibility of
getting scales in the climate chamber registering the weight automatically was investigated,
Withdrawal of punched metal plate fasteners in trussed rafters
47
==PAGE:56==
but not possible to obtain within the time frame of the project.
5.2.4.3
Practical setup and performance of test
As explained in section 5.2.4.1, the small climate chamber is set up with the needed supplies.
It has been set up to be used without potential external loads, hence a fully closed chamber
is accessible. Further, the water supply of the chamber has been connected so the RH could
be included in the test. At the beginning of the test, the 3 selected workpieces were in
moisture balance and weighted, and the chamber was turned on to have stable conditions
of a temperature of 20 ℃and RH of 50%. The 3 workpieces were placed in the chamber
with good space between each other and the walls of the chamber, hence securing the
airflow to make similar temperature and RH in the chamber. The setup of the workpieces
occurs in figure 35.
Figure 35: Set up of workpieces small climate chamber
The performance of the test primarily consisted of observations of the chamber to secure
that the conditions in the chamber were still stable, taking photos to document the eventual
48
Withdrawal of punched metal plate fasteners in trussed rafters
==PAGE:57==
withdrawal of the PMPF, and measuring the weight. The weighing of the workpieces made
an opportunity to keep an eye on the withdrawal of all PMPF in the workpieces. When
the weight of the workpieces started to flatten out on a curve of the weight over time,
and thereby almost reach the maximum or minimum weight in the present conditions,
the conditions in the chamber were changed. In the changing phase a concentration of
observations thinking of climate chamber conditions and workpiece weight were made,
hence the largest changes were expected to happen there.
The RH started at 50%, and after reaching acceptable weights the conditions were changed
to 85%.
Unfortunately, a problem with the supply of water to the chamber occurred
reaching a high percentage of MC of the workpieces.
Therefore, the workpieces were
affected by low RH and thereby were drying out. The machine was fixed by the staff at
the structural lab, and in the meantime, the workpieces were relocated to the climate room
with stable conditions of 20 ℃and 65% RH. The climate chamber worked well after being
turned off for some hours. The routine was repeated, but unfortunate problems with the
RH occurred again. The problem was attempted to be solved by turning off the machine
again, but this was not obtained within a short time. At this point, the time frame was
about to run out, hence it was not assessed possible to reach the high RH and thereby a
high MC before the end of the project. Though, the workpieces have been exposed to both
high and low RH, so the results are presented and analyzed in section 6.3.

6
Results and analysis
The section is divided into three subsections based on the tests: Test 1 in BUILD, test 2
at DTU with varying temperature, and test 3 at DTU with varying relative humidity, RH.
Test 1 includes management of the data and the corresponding processing of the data. For
tests 2 and 3, the conditions the workpieces are affected by are presented and evaluated.
Further, the observations of respectively moisture content, MC, weight, and movements
are described.
6.1
Test 1: Test house
Test 1 is performed as explained in section 5.1 of which the data outcome are handled
within the present section. Afterward, the data is processed into graphs comparing for
example the conditions at the peak of the roof with conditions at the lower points. Data
for temperatures and RH are three months: March, April, and May, while MC occur during
the three months in the interval 16th of March to 20th of May.
6.1.1
Consideration of data management
The data from the trusses consist of one temperature and RH each hour for each measuring
point over one day in three months. The temperature typically fluctuation relatively large
over a day. It can be hard to see through the data and spot the tendency by looking at a
longer period. Similarly, the RH fluctuate over time. Moving average, MA, is used to see
a more general development over a longer period than a day.
The EMA method is used and occur as the light green curve at the figures 36 and 37.
The curve tends to be stable, hence it has smaller peaks than the daily average and SMA-
method curves. It only increase and decrease slowly, which causes the extremes to peak
a bit later than the daily average and the SMA-method curves at figure 37. Additionally,
the number of peaks is highly reduced compared to the daily average and the SMA method
at that figure. On the other hand, the peaks are similar in figure 36, though the SMA
methods are still more extreme than EMA. Since the EMA method has 10% influence on
the current value and 90% influence on the previous value it is reasonable that the curve
peaks slower and has fewer peak points than the SMA methods.
An average of each date is calculated and occur at the figure as the ”Daily average” in
pink. The average reduce the amount of noise in terms of variation, but the tendency can
be more clear. Therefore, based on this average, the SMA method is used through Excel
and occurs as ”10 per. glid. gnsn.” in dark red at figure 36. A period of 10 is chosen so
the curve reaches a stable level but starts as early as possible in the time frame. In figure
37 the period is shortened, hence the curve starts only 1 hour late. At the same time, the
curve gets more unstable and gets close to the Daily average curve, so the tendency gets
harder to read from the graph. Therefore, 10 is assessed more usable for the purpose of
50
Withdrawal of punched metal plate fasteners in trussed rafters
==PAGE:59==
the analysis.
Similarly, the SMA method is used for the daily data, which appears as the blue curve
in the figures. In figure 36 the period is 200 and the curve consists of small waves, but
overall, the shape is like the SMA of the daily average. As the period value decrease, the
curve turns out to be like the daily average and SMA of the daily average in figure 37.
The indications of all methods point in the same direction, hence the EMA method is
assessed as true and fair for showing the tendency of the development of for example the
temperature.
Figure 36: Moving average M1.1.07
Figure 37: Moving average M1.1.07

6.2
Test 2: Joints affected by varying temperatures with constant relative
humidity
Test 2 is performed as explained in section 5.2.3 of which the results occur in the present
section with corresponding analysis. Different approaches occur consisting of the conditions
in the test, the MC of the joints, and the documentation of the joints.
6.2.1
Conditions
The large climate chamber has been started on the 19th of April and stopped 12th of June.
Two times the climate chamber has been stopped by power fails and in total almost three
days. In the three days, it is assessed that the RH was relatively high, hence two days were
measured. The climate chamber stopped at the peak of temperature. The RH did not
get stable at 65% before the machine stopped, which is known from the data measured at
the external measuring equipment, which occur in figure 55. Over time, the temperature
decreased slowly from 61.5 ℃to 30.0 ℃, while the RH were at 76.3% and ended at 80.2%.
During the decreasing temperature, the RH reached a peak at 83.6%. The same conditions
are assessed to be reasonable for the third day since the conditions were likely when it was
discovered that the climate chamber had stopped again. There, the temperature was 30.1
℃and RH was 74%. The curves at the climate chamber showed the same tendency, hence
stable but high RH and decreasing temperature.
Figure 55: Conditions from 19.05 to 23.05, external sensor
The large climate chamber obtained just above 308 cycles of 4-hour cycles with 20℃as the
lowest temperature and 60℃as the highest temperature according to the internal sensors
of the climate chamber. The cycles that are documented with the external sensors occur
in appendix J. The values of the temperature and RH, and relation between the internal
sensors and the external sensors were the same during the test with the workpieces inside
the climate chamber. Therefore, the climate chamber did have the capacity to facilitate
the test.

the test. The workpieces were not measured during the test, since the MC and weight are
expected to be relatively stable during the test, thinking of the small difference of EMC ex-
plained in section 5.2.3. Selected workpieces were weighed with the same considerations as
explained in the preparation of the workpieces in section 5.2.1.3. The weights of workpiece
2, 4, and 27 occur in table 16. Since the weight was 1-2% smaller at the end, the weights
were measured after two days in the climate room to see if the weight would increase again.
The weights did increase again, hence some variation of either the climate chamber or the
climate room might occur. Though the variation is small, it might be caused by the time
of stopping the test, the small variation of EMC, or measuring uncertainties. Therefore,
the conditions are evaluated to be similar.

seem to do it before the curves have flattened out totally, hence the starting point might
be too low for them. On the other hand, workpiece 33 does not reach the same low level,
hence only approximately 10% MC, hence the starting point might be too high for this
workpiece. The same tendency continues as the intervals occur. Workpiece 6 seems to
reach a higher MC than workpiece 18 at the peaks, but still way lower than workpiece 33.
Since the climate chamber failed at a high level of RH, the highest level of MC ended being
15.5% for workpiece 33, while it was 13.3% for workpiece 18 and 18.8% for workpiece 6.
